RATING: 8.5
I really enjoyed this latest offering from Mac Gerdts. This time out, the 'additional' actions cost more than just a coin - they cost one of your important assets: a ship. It makes for a more limited game and is better for it. Also, I really like the smooth phasing and ramping of costs - I even think the market works just fine. I imagine this game doesn't offer too many strategy lanes, but still, it is very tight and smart. Certainly true that experienced players will lose because of seating order or any one (minor) misplay. It's a knife walk. Perhaps too tight? Need more plays to tell.

RATING: 3
Sometimes, when playing board games, I wish I could be a little bit more like Peter Pan. You know, with the blink of an eye I could move to another world. A world where the elements of fantasy come to life, where everything is possible and ideas have wings. 
Unfortunately, I am not like Peter Pan. With some games, when someone tries to explain the rules to me, I just get annoyed and wonder what the hell the designer was thinking. And, unfortunately, Navegador is one of those games. So ... you have those little cardboard pieces that you can exchange for little wooden thingies which you can exchange for other cardboard pieces which you can exchange for other wooden thingies which gives you points. Sigh. And all this is a journey to the magical world of ... wait, I KNOW I can remember the theme of the game, if I only try hard enough...
So, once again, I would love to be like Peter Pan, because then games like Navegador would probably start to be fun. Unfortunately, I am more the Captain Hook-type (and age), so this first round of Navagador will probably be my last.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I really like Navegador. While the graphics and components are nice - I especially like how the board looks -, the game-play is where this game shines. This was my first game with the famous rondel mechanic by Mac Gerdts and I immediately fell in love with it. It's just so elegant! I can't believe how many different strategies this mechanism provides from such a limited amount of choices. Turns run really fast and smooth, because of the simplicity of the actions, but the game is still very tense... 

I tried three rondel games at this point and I can say, that this is particularly true in Navegador. You sit there waiting, hoping that the privilege tile or those colonies that you want will still be there when the turn comes back to you. There's a lot of indirect conflict. Taking pieces away from others is really important, but the center of this conflict is the market. Manage to control it at your advantage and you'll really be able to boost your engine and run away with the game. I think this Market mechanism is the best and simplest implementation of this concept in all the games I played so far. 

So, summing up... Even if I don't enjoy Navegador as much as other Rondel games, I still like this game a lot. It's clever, elegant and very fun even if it doesn't hit the table so often. Good job, Mr. Gerdts!

RATING: 8
[b]18.12.2014 - after 1 play[/b]
Navegador was one of games high on my Want-To-Play list, probably due to the praise many people give to it. This is my second rondel game (after ANTIKE, which was ok).

The components of the game are not too fancy, neither too bleach, with a nice mix of wooden components (ships/buidings), nicely drawn map and ugly tiles. The theme of territory exploration / colony building goes together wonderful with the gameplay, almost all the actions having a theme association. The rules are easy to learn/teach and after 1-2 turns every thing comes into place.

Gameplay-wise, this game (as other rondel games) consists of small actions played until a final condition is reached (here, the discovery of the Nagasaki territory or the depletion of all the buildings). Although there are many options, the limitations of the rondel make for a game with very little downtime. We played a 5-player game (all the players being new to the game, with only one reading/explaining the rules) in just 2 hours (rules explanation included). 

I liked the ability for combos (encouraged by the Navegador card) and the fact that you can follow different paths to victory. Also, these paths make me wonder if there is some replayabily in this game. I consider this to be an almost perfect information game and I fear that after playing some times, you can find a "winning" solution for the victory. 

Anyway, a good game which I want to play more to try another approach.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[i]2017/06/03 - 1 play 5 players[/i]

Very clean, fast playing rondel game, with  clearly deliminated early-middle-end games. Even at five players there is no downtime, turns play briskly.

Compared to Concordia, there are similarities in the end-scoring building and the Navegador card which accelerates the game flow. Contrary to Concordia all the information is open and the scoring of each player is transparent during play. On the other hand there is no build-up of actions through cards, only the same rondel from the beginning to the end.

The market is a nice addition, and allows a lot of interaction between players. For instance, seat position can be important depending on who is importing/consuming resources.

RATING: 7
Very attractive Euro-game! We had some rule issues and decided the following: (1) Navigator can be used before the owner's turn (not anyone's turn), (2) No need to have a colony to process a good. 

In our 5 player game, the game was kind of dull and some players didn't care for it (including hubby). The winner did no navigation or exploration, and stuck with the market strategy (and he found that boring). 

But, I still think this is one of the better Euro-style games with a stronger, better theme than Endeavor and as good as what I remember Cuba to be from a mechanics perspective. The fact that it has a great map, fast and easy game flow, icons as instruction reminders, a quick reference sheet, and the rondel make it all the better. At the very least, the best Portuguese themed game we've played. Tracked on family list (grooven)!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Contrary to what you might think, this is not, in essence, an exploration game: it is far more an economic game. If you want an exploration game, play Entdecker.
This is a very good, and extremely challenging, game.
At first glance, it might appear to be multi-player solitaire, but it's not: everything YOU do has an impact on everyone else, and vice versa. And a winning strategy MUST depend on constant awareness of what your opponents are doing.
For a eurogame, the theme is very well portrayed, not least of which is because of the gorgeous antique map of a board. Well themed, but not immersively themed: you will not feel like a Renaissance age Portuguese explorer, nor even a Portuguese merchant, for that matter. Rather, you will feel remarkably like a 21st century eurogamer playing a reasonably well themed eurogame.
The only real fear I have for this game is the issue of replayability, due to the rondel. Since almost everything in Navegador is open information, will the game become rather scripted with experienced players? And if the players are not experienced, there is much room for analysis paralysis.

RATING: 5
The Age of Discovery - what an exciting theme for a game.  Never knowing what's over the horizon, whether your food and fresh water will last until landfall, whether your ship will survive the next storm; finally, the thrill of disovery.  

Forget all that, Navegador is just another low luck euro efficiency game.  Everything is calculable and quite, quite boring.  The rule about automatically losing one and just one ship when exploring uncharted waters says it all for me. It's like Mac Gerdts is at your shoulder the whole time, whispering "It's just a game, it's just a game."

The artwork is quite nice and production quality is good.  The rules seem clear and concise.

RATING: 9
Very entertaining, fast-playing optimization/economic game with compelling theme and very good art and components. The market price system is especially solid, as demand for raw and processed goods is tracked as a whole, leading to interesting buy/sell dynamics without a lot of fiddliness. Overall the design feels extremely tight -- everything has its role in the play, yet nothing feels cloned.  Hard to imagine a scenario where I wouldn't want to put this on the table. Highly recommended.

RATING: 7
Initial 4P play took for-ev-er, around 3 hours.  Light investments in both churches and shipyards made developing the game financially painful, and once players were in a position to end it everyone was drawing down the building stack as they didn't know how far ahead or behind they might be.  I knew I was behind, so I kept slogging.  Lousy game experience there.  I played it again with another 4P group, and it flew by in perhaps 75 minutes.  Much more pleasant.  Easily my second-favorite rondel game I've played, just behind Imperial and distantly ahead of both Hamburgum and Antike.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The opening turns feel a bit chaotic as players settle into their strategies. Workable strategies can be based on what the preceding player is doing, so you can leech of how that player affects the market. Once the initial turns are through, the game stabilizes into a fairly procedural mid-game march. The procedure of the mid-game can be interrupted for tactical play (e.g., if a player sees an opening to disrupt an opponent or grab a side-benefit). The end game can drag out ever slightly if there's a player unwilling to take that last explore (or build action) to end the game -- this is exacerbated a bit by the hidden scoring. All that said, it plays smoothly and is fairly satisfying in both table presence and game play. For a pure Euro-style game, it has a decent marriage of game play and theme, too.

However, this is a game that would be rated higher if I played it closer to its release date or if I played it before other more recently released games. Navegador is definitely a well designed game: the rule set is well crafted, the scoring is balanced, and it requires players to find a strategy that works for that play session. Navegador's resource market and well-considered package will get this played sporadically. But I'm not sure I'd consistently play this over other games that are broadly similar (e.g., Concordia, Great Western Trail, Voyages of Marco Polo). You could use this game with players that feel more intimidated by heavier games (e.g., Brass) since the rondel does a good job of simplifying action selection.
 
Add-ons/Expansions/Extras:
Navegador: Privilege Cards (2016)
Navegador: Pirates & Diplomats (2012)

Update: Unfortunately, this one just doesn't do it for me. You eke out victory points over the course of the game, and unless you spend the time (which drags the game down) to calculate the relative places of the players, the winner is fairly anticlimactic.

Original: Obvious similarities to Imperial, but without the stocks to create shared incentives. RATING: 7.11

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I liked it, overall.  It's not "never turn down a game"(8), it's better than 'ok'(6), definitely better than 'tolerate it'(5).

The good:  I liked that I felt like I had a decent enough grasp of it after my 2nd or 3rd turn.  So it's easy to learn for the (sort-of) thickness of a euro it is.  It's hard to understate this point, because it did have some economy and depth to it---and I felt like a pro before the halfway point.  That's huge.  I also liked that while it was hard to behave offensively, and switch-up a strategy midgame, it wasn't impossible to find some really worthwhile actions involving the other players.  It was absolutely NOT multi-player solitaire.

The bad:  I have the same problem that I have with a lot of other resource euros.  It will appear, and you can think to yourself that you know what's going on.  Looking closer, there are so many options and things laid before you, I don't see any way you could possibly know.  So why all this facade with the rules when it's 70% knowing what to do, and 30% being in the right place at the right time?  Quite a bit of the strategy depended on knowing what the other players were doing, and planning accordingly.  That's good, and it gives some weight to it being a solid, interactive resource game. However, some of it happens to be finding yourself in the right place at the right time with the right gambits.
------------------------------------
In rating this game, I asked myself, "What would I do differently next game?"  and all I could come up with is maybe not making a costly 'mistake' at the beginning of the game by going exploring my first turn out, and maybe looking to see what the guy ahead of me in turn order found himself doing.  Which is true for most resource games, but with this one in particular, there's more nuances and things that score points for a person to keep track of to really feel any accurate sense of competition with eachother until the very end of the game.  So it's like all the resource management of a good resource euro, but none of the sport of tactical plays purposefully targeting one opponent or another for any reason other than, "hey, they look like they're in the lead".  

Objective game comments:  Overall, a good design that really explained itself and the theme, rondel, and limited ruleset all seemed to tie the game together.  My only knock against it, is there are some nuanced rules that stay hidden because they rarely come up, like selling excess items/workers for money.  

I don't know how they would fix it, but having a score track that updates relatively close to real-time rather than the end of the game seriously would take this 7 into an 8.  The one game I played I think was separated by like 10% between first and last, and knowing whether to extend the game, shut it down, or target another player a little more clearly would have really raised the tension up quite a bit.  This game had all the tools to do each, but it was difficult to deeply read into it.

I realize that there are very few games with a perfect scoring track, but I would like to have some basis of belief to help determine who is ahead, because accurately determining who is ahead in this game is pretty much impossible based on an eyeball of strong plays, etc.  Again, with this one game we were all within a pretty close range of eachother, like 10 points.  Considering it was 126-ish for first, and last might have been like 109, there's no way you could really make an accurate guess as to who to take-down.  Any game I enjoy is one where, from time-to-time, it's best to kick your opponent in the nuts rather than outbuild him.

RATING: 8
The scoring system clashing with the central game mechanic is what makes this game so fantastic. The central game mechanic obviously being the rondel, forces players to diversify, because they are not able to take the same action multiple times in a row. The scoring system on the other hand favors players specializing in one or two specific areas to maximize the total score. Therefore, the key to winning the game is to find ways around this contradiction.

Brilliant system.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
I really enjoy this game. The rondel mechanism keeps the turns flowing very quickly and smoothly. The market dynamics are simple and work perfectly, creating a wonderful balance between producing and selling. The timing decisions about when to pick privileges or when to save up workers are great. Just like Concordia, this game just feels like a classic euro in all respects. The rules are simple, the decisions are fun, and the game play is silky smooth. While most of the setup is static, it still feels like there are several viable options and a large number of point scoring combinations to explore. In addition, you will need to adjust your strategy based on how things are playing out early in the game. Very glad I added this to my collection.

Note: We have played 2 player with Tom Lehmann’s 2 player variant (remove a few colonies) and it seems well balanced and works nicely. I would recommend this.

RATING: 7.3
As a Rondel game fan, I was gonna like this. This one's different from the Antike/Imperial ones though as there's less direct conflict and more exploration. There's a bunch of different "Build" items to buy which act as multipliers for the other actions on the rondel. i.e. when you land on "Ships" you build 1 cheap ship for every shipyard you have purchased from the "Build" action. These also get used in scoring at the end. There's also bonuses for exploring sea regions, founding colomies and so on, this is a good game for picking a strategy no-one else is using and making it work. Best to have a second strategy as well, as just one is not usually enough to win.

RATING: 10
Another clever rondel game from Mac Gerdts. Perhaps even slicker than the "Imperials," this game moves quickly and provides enough decision points to keep your interest, despite being relatively simple and easy to pick up. There is very little downtime and, with three players, comes in under two hours. All the moving parts fit together seamlessly and the market mechanic seems to work well - it will be interesting to learn whether four or five players will change this significantly.

The VP track around the board will concern some players until they see that it only comes into play during the final scoring at game end. On the other hand, watching the actions of your opponents will give you a pretty good idea how everyone is doing. It turns out that the accumulation of victory points is quite transparent once you see how the final scoring works. For those worried about such things, it will come down to whether you prefer to play with open or closed money.

Rondel games have their charm but some have complained that the "Imperials" suffer from the mid to late-game "spin-cycle" which must simply be endured as by then the winner has pretty well become apparent. So far, a similar phenomena has not been seen here. I've quite enjoyed the games I've played so far and look forward to more plays, particularly with more players. My one concern is that the game will "play itself out" sooner than other more complex games.

Edit Note - One thing that should be mentioned here in its favour is that it is an extremely good game for three players, which the Imperials are not. One might even think this game was designed for three.

Further Note -I've had the chance now to play with four and five players and am happy to report that the playing time did not go up appreciatively and while quite a different game (more likely to have the end triggered by building depletion as player numbers increase), it was no less fun and interesting to play. I've decided that it scales very well to the number of players which is another plus in its favour.

And yet another note - As I return yet again to this title I no longer worry that it will wear out its welcome. It is a wonderful game for three, four or five players and is my favorite Rondel game of the lot...

RATING: 7
2-5 | (4) | 90 min | 12+ | Mac Gerdts
Navegador is the paradigm of solid eurogames: excellent components, few rules with no room for exceptions, short playtime, close scores and multiple paths to victory. One of its strengths is the little to no downtime between turns, a pattern shared by all rondel games. The rondel actions seem well balanced and work like clockwork. In this case, the key to winning seems to identify and then follow the path of least resistance, so you do not compete for the different bonus tiles.

My biggest quibble is the oversized box, which won't fit anywhere.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Wow. 

This might be Mac Gerdt's best rondel game, besting Antike.

A pure efficiency/economics game. Lots of potential different paths. Timing seems critical in this game. You gotta know when to sell vs process, which buildings to buy. Going to the market to maximize your income based on what opponents are doing and NOT doing. Definitely not multi-player solitaire. If you do not carefully watch what your opponents are doing/not doing, then you will lose. 

This is truly one of the most balanced, multiple paths to victory game that I have ever experienced. Unlike in so many euros, getting the most actions or having the best money generating engine doesn't get you the win. 

And to top it all off, the game is highly thematic.

Fantastic!

The best of Essen 2010.

The last few turns are just auto-pilot wheel spinning, no epic feel, build up or excitement.

RATING: 9
Initial rating after 1 play: 9.  Fantastic game -- the best new game I've played in a long time.   Nice to see market mechanics combined with multiple paths to victory.

Jan 2011:  Upped to a 10, at least for now.  I suspect over time I will get bored of it and the rating will drop, but I am continued to be impressed by how clean the design of this game is.  No mechanics are wasted or are unnecessary.

Sep 2011:  Rating down to a 9.  Don't have the desire to play this that often, although I do still really like it.  Not sure why.  Perhaps it's because it takes some mental effort to try to remember how to play each time.

RATING: 8
Finally getting around to commenting on this game.   First, it's very pretty,and the rondel mechanism really works well here.  There is a sort of a race going on, but not all players need to participate in it to have a shot at winning.  It's definitely a game where you need to distance yourself from the crowd.   If someone is going after a strategy, you need to find your own thing,or you will be hampering each other all game long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is my pick for "favorite Mac Gerdts rondel game."  Imperial is probably his best game, but for two it was a little too much work for the fun payoff, and with more it is longer than I'd like.  Basically, I admire Imperial more than I enjoy playing it.  I enjoy Princes of Macchu Picchu, but the gold victory is out of whack and makes the game more one-sided than I'd like.

Navegador, on the other hand, is a great combination between a simple yet deep rondel mechanic and a game of procedural geography similar to many Winsome games.  Basically, you try to maximize your VP by doing certain things - building colonies, factories, churches, etc.  What's great is that you can collect multiplies for these categories, and these are limited in availability.  The rules of the game are fairly straightforward and easy, as are the rondel actions - turns move fast.  There are two ways to make money (somewhat at odds with each other) but money is just the key to buying getting ahead in your specialized areas.  The pace of the game is set by the map - once you've opened up the full progression from Portugal to Japan, the game is over.  This, coupled with the rondel, makes for a game where timing is crucial.  The game plays very well with two, moreso than any of the other rondel games - I do use the [thread=671709]Tom Lehman variant[/thread] though.  A super solid Euro.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Wonderful, tense, tight game. There are several paths to victory, but you have to do a little of everything to stay competitive. The heart of the game lies in figuring out the right balance between specializing in one area and doing well in everything else. What other players do has a direct impact on which path you choose, which makes this an incredibly exciting game. 

The rondel is simply awesome. Since each action is important, and timing can often be crucial, it can be very tricky deciding whether to jump forward on the rondel or linger behind. The rondel, however, also enables you to see what is available to other players, which makes each of your own decisions even harder: do you use the Navegador extra action now, even though it might benefit the other player on their next turn? Should you colonize a sugar plantation, even though someone else bought a bunch of sugar and are likely to drive down the price? From the very first turn the game takes on a very tense and thinky dimension that lasts throughout the entire game. A truly wonderful design.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
3/22/16: 3 plays. My second Mac Gerdts game (Concordia being the first) that I really enjoy. I have come to really like the scoring of points being done during the end game as it adds some suspense and surprise to the final outcome.  You don't necessarily feel you're out of it until the end.

5/14/16: Upped my rating to 8.5.   One of my top 20 games.

RATING: 7.5
Great game! I really like the rondel mechanic, it's a unique element; well, I know there are many other games with a rondel, but it's not THAT common compared to other mechanics, and I hadn't tried any other games with a rondel before.

The mechanics are very well thought out and interlocked in a way I think only few games can match. Once you know the rules, it's actually a very easy gameplay; easy to learn, hard to master. This also means that it's one of those games you might be able to introduce to new players after you've played Ticket to Ride and Carcassonne and such (we have done so with success!), but experienced gamers will still enjoy it a lot.

Overall it's a game I enjoy and which has lots of paths to victory which is always a huge plus. It is true, though, that it's hard to win if you are using the same strategy as an other player, especially if it's the player to your right, while others have a strategy for themselves, but overall I still enjoy this game for it's simple yet deep mechanics. However, after repeated plays it seems that the paths to victory are sort of "fixed"; you either focus on, for instance, churches and factories, or you focus on shipyards and exploring and such.

I gave it a rating of 8.5 after two plays or so, but after having played the game 7 times I have decreased my rating slightly; it's still a very good game, it's just not one I want to play all the time.

RATING: 9
Navegador is easily one of the more exciting games I've played that I wasn't actively hunting down to own myself. I really enjoy Imperial 2030, so I was keen to see how that rondel worked in another game. It certainly does with this one.

Both Imperial 2030 and Navegador share something I love, which is the benefits of the rondel. The rondel provides simplicity in turn order (i.e. choose your rondel space--execute the action--next!), but the angst in wanting to do everything but move onto the next juicy action. Where they diverge is that Imperial 2030 is a stock game, and Navegador is really a tech-tree game of sorts.

Navegador's map is beautiful. And, I love how the game gradually progresses across the board. There seems to be multiple paths to victory. I was just simply impressed by this game and very much look forward to playing it again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I imagine if I played this years ago before Concordia I would have bought it instantly as it really is quiet clever. Move 1-3 spaces on the rondel and do the action of the space you land on. Each space does something different and all the actions intertwine together well so I can see how you can execute a slightly different strategy each game if you want although it does feel a bit more limited. Between the linear map, goods in same location, scoring multipliers, and rondel actions it all feels so similar to Concordia which has all the variability built into it. Lastly, I have found in a second play that the person who has gone after a scoring objective with minimal interaction from others has won the game both times, this forces a diverse strategy which is not apparent to everyone on the first play. I would definitely be willing to play this game again as it is a solid euro game, but for my collection I will gladly keep Concordia.

RATING: 8
I think this is the most subtly elegant of the rondel games to date. I didn't appreciate the beauty of it immediately, but there are some nice elements.  Most notable is the distribution of actions on the rondel itself. 

In some of the prior games, there were some channels carved out that you'd perform repetitive cycles. It was not too hard to predict what players were going to do based on rondel position. In this game, every single action you skip is slightly painful. But there are enough varied strategies and flexibility that you can alter your choice based on what players around you are doing. I don't want to overstate that point, but I do think the rondel itself is a lot more delicately balanced than in past games.

The theme seems to work for me. The progression through exploration is visually intuitive. 

I had lukewarm expectations of this one, but it surprised me.

RATING: 7
Update after a dozen+ plays:
This is a vp game that rewards specialization. That's good. Unfortunately, it's also an economic engine game, and you are completely hosed if you try to develop the same or similar engine to the player to your right. You don't need to comit to the vp specialization until after your economy is rolling. And if your economy doesn't get rolling, you're toast, with no possible path of recovery. This isn't nearly as multi-dimensional as it first appeared. Rating bumped down a bit.
-----
Very clean system. Extremely low randomness. I don't say that purely as a good thing, but it is what it is. Those who like planning things out should be comfortable here. The main unknown is the chaos of other player's. Nice supply/demand market system. There's a strong motivation to base what you're producing/selling to be opposite of the player before you. As there are three types of goods, it is best as a 4p for optimal tension. With 3p each player can produce and sell non-conflicting goods as other players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
If it works, don't fix it. That should be Mac Gerdts' motto, if it isn't his motto already. The fact that he is shamelessly reusing the same mechanism time and time again, with very little variation in between games, is beginning to look suspiciously like laziness at his design end. Navegador is a 'simple' rondel game (like Hamburgum and Princes of Macchu Pichu) in that actions on the rondel have short and well-defined consequences; this in sharp contrast to Antike where 'movement' can be a long game-changing ordeal. And compared to the other short rondel games, Navegador actually looks ergnomically acceptable too. The game itself is a familiar mix of things influencing each other so that you really want to do everything on the rondel. And just like any other rondel game, there is the sensation of walking on a stair with uneven steps. Especially Navegador is about chosing a method of playing and then sticking to it more religiously than a fundamentalist to his scripture. Small errors can cost you the game here. 

That all said, Navegador isn't all simplicity. The market mechanism makes a left-right binding very important; you can really wreak havoc with a player's immediate future prospects by doing exactly what he does as long as you are on his right. As a result you should always try to do something everyone else isn't (or is so far away that the play order effects are washed out somewhat) and then Navegador begins to feel constrained rather quickly. When the game is up and running it is, apart from the blasted rondel, a fine game to play... But there simply can't be much variation between games because of the need of everyone doing something else, and the rondel not presenting that much variety. I  probably will play Navegador in the future again on occasion, but I also think that these plays will be all that I need to satisfy my curiosity. If I ignore the rondel the game is worth a 6 to me; but since I can't, it means that Navegador gets one point deduction.

RATING: 7
Components: Lovely old-style board; lots of wooden bits; chunky money. Given how central the rondel is, it should perhaps be a bit larger.

Experience: Wow, there is a lot going on in this game. I made the early mistake of thinking this is a game like Agricola, where I need to keep up in each aspect, whereas the game moreso favors those who specialize in a few types of scoring. This speaks well for some near-term replayability, that there are different strategic paths to test.

It's difficult to predict which actions other players will take, and therefore how the state of the board will change before my next turn; I find myself just planning out my own next moves, and hoping that the board favors those decisions when it's my turn.

Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

Plays (1):
5 players: 1

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Navegador is a solid game.  The rules are straight forward, and there are a lot of choices  You can use hard earned resources (ships) to get an edge in future turns.  There are also limited amounts of Privileges to fight over for victory point multipliers.  

It takes a complete play to see how it all fits together, and how to make all your efforts stack together for the most efficient point engine.  You could certainly play many times without ever using the same strategy.

With all that said, I though the game was a little flat.  I liked being able to do a lot of different things, but it just wasn't all that exciting.  I think a hidden scoring element would help a lot.  I don't like it went you can glance at the table at getting a pretty good idea who is in the lead.

It's worth a try to decide for yourself.  It is a solid game, but not a keeper for me.

RATING: 7.9
Players choose actions on a rondel in order to build shipyards in order to build ships to explore the Oceans as Portuguese seafarers, build colonies, produce commodities in these colonies and sell these to the market, build factories to produce finished products and sell these to the market, and...build churches, which used to be necessary to attract a work force. Points are scored per colony, factory, explored sea, shipyard and church. These numbers are multiplied by a certain amount of points which can be influenced by obtaining privileges (extra points) by killing a worker. Yes, these were strange and cruel times. Each worker and ship on the board at the end of the game also equals 1 point, as does each full 200 Cruzados (the monetary unit). Prices of commodities (sugar, gold, spices) go down when a new load arrives and is sold on the market. Concurrently, the price of the finished good which is based on this commodity rises. When finished goods are sold, their price falls, while the price of the corresponding commodity increases. Prices are in other words purely supply driven. There is no link with demand for the commodities by the factories. It is not even necessary to have a colony producing spices in order to sell dried spices in jars from your factories. So, the market mechanism is a bit peculiar, but it does allow for fast price changes.
Overall, the game plays very fluently. Downtime while waiting for other players to take a decision is minimal, because the logical choices on the rondel are fairly clear, since you can only take 3 steps max...unless you start burning your ships...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
$75 Amazon
This game is inspired by the Portuguese Age of Discoveries in the 15th-16th century. Players take actions such as contracting men, acquiring ships and buildings, sailing the seas, establishing colonies in discovered lands, trading goods on the market, and getting privileges. Each player starts with only two ships and three workers and tries to expand his wealth.
There are several undiscovered lands that allow players, once discovered, to found some colonies there.
At the end of the game the player who is most successful in combining his privileges with his achievements (colonies, factories, discoveries, shipyards, and churches) is the winner.

RATING: 7.4
	
The rondel is a cool mechanic of the game and really its major asset.

The theme (charting the unknown seas) is also nice and quite appealling for portuguese players.
The game develops in a logical way, gathering resources and making use of the market price flutuations, however, some privileges seem quite unbalanced (churches, for instance) and the discover actions seem to be too much penalized, since you have to throw away an entire boat and submit to whatever the new land brings to you.
It is also usual that the most advanced player on the map is not the one who has more points, so he delays the game end (not reaching Japan), hoping to catch up, but that proves quite hard to achieve, so the game continues for more 15/30 min with no change whatsoever in the lead.
Wrapping up, it´s a game that generates some interest around the theme and some of its mechanics but, on the other hand, suffers from some rough edges in its rules.

RATING: 9
In this surprisingly fast-paced game, actions are chosen via a rondel mechanic. You can explore, produce goods, colonize, sell, build ships and increase your workforce. Each of these things works differently with the others, making for many viable victory strategies.

However, depending on what other players do, you're better off being flexible. Instead of doing the same thing as them, try to use what they're doing to your benefit. (So, if the player right before you is focussing on exploring, you can tag along behind and colonize all his newly-explored regions. There are many such strategies in this great game.)

RATING: 7
Yet another Rondel.
This has a multitude of ways to score points, but specialisation is important given multipliers can be purchased.
The board looks great, especially since its essentially just a linear track with a few tables tacked on. (Yes the first few spaces have cross conenctions, but then its just a race track.)
I was disappointed with the mechanism for advancing further on the rondel (ship discard) since these is an exorbitant price. I can see it rarely being useful which makes it a waste of rule space and removes some tactical flexibility.
I do also feel - given the multiplier and collection of tiles/chits - that lucking out on a specialisation that none of your opponents is pursuing is more important than executing a well crafted strategy. Yes, you can keep your options open to then take what no one else is going for, but if you happen to fall into something alone on day 1, you're probably going to win.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ninety percent of the scoring in this game is akin to the 'sand bottom' scoring in Stone Age. You choose from five different items to collect and try to accumulate the multipliers that coordinate best with your collection. I liked that idea in Stone Age, and I like it here.
The only randomness in the game is the order in which the colonies are revealed, in much the same way as the plantations provide a minimal random element in Puerto Rico. I wonder whether playing against Navegador's adepts eventually will prove as discouraging as playing against Puerto Rico experts.

RATING: 7
This is a very enjoyable 90 minutes game.  There is a lot of think about, but your options are always constrained to 1 of 3 possible actions, which makes the game move at a good pace.  This might be my favorite of the rondel games, which is saying a lot since I was already a big fan of Imperial.

It has a similar feel to Puerto Rico in that you are constantly watching to see what the person to your left is doing (you don't want to do the same thing as them), but it has a simpler learning curve since there are not a whole bunch of buildings that you can buy.

I have only played it once, so I cannot say for certain how much re-playability the game has, but it seems to have significant depth and many viable strategies.

RATING: 9
I played my first two games yesterday and today. Second game was better than the first. The market action takes some effort to figure, but I think we have come up with a way to keep that fiddyness to a minimum. Both games were very tight in score. First game was a tie for the win at 60 something and the third player being only 2 points less. The tie breaker being the holder of the Navegador (or the next tied player to get it if the holder isn't the winner) was kinda hollow for the amount of time put into the game. The second game we scored over double the points of the first, with first and last place only being 6 points difference. A very, very balanced and tight game. It makes me wonder what a person needs to do to "pull away from the pack". The first game took 2.5 hours and the second took 3 hours to finish. The box sites 45-90 minutes which I believe is way off, even as we get more comfortable with the mechanics.

RATING: 7
I'll start this at an 8.  I don't typically like Rondel systems that much.  Often I feel like the paths become very static...1st half of the game take spots A, C, and E...2nd half take B, D, and F.  While this game has some of this, it is not a pronounced pattern.  Sometimes you really want to go to every spot and it comes down to which spots *must* you have to get the VPs necessary to win.  It also has a pretty cool economic mechanic whereby you use factories to raise the price of goods and you sell goods to drop the price.

But the downside of this cool mechanic is the that I'm not sure it makes sense thematically.  Thus I had trouble figuring out exactly how it worked together.  A couple trips to the market will make it all clear though.

On the negative side, the market does swing up and down a lot...so it's hard to predict a pattern, use strategy and cash in.  Mostly you are at the whim of players going before you.  Still, it's not a huge difference maker either way.

Collecting factories seems very strong...that combined with 5 multiplier in the privilege category produced a lot of VPs...all while still maintaining economic momentum.  Hopefully it isn't too strong a strategy.

Figure the final rating will be between 8 (where I put i now) and 6.

Update: As expected, this one has dropped a bit.  Moving it down to a 7.  I think it has a low ceiling with not too much replayability.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[ImageID=855277]

Navegador, the latest in a long line of rondel games by Mac Gerdts, is a game focused on the exploration of and exploitation of the resources of South America, Africa, and Asia. It has many parallels to Hamburgum, but is described as many as an improvement and upgrade on that less well-regarded game. I have not played Hamburgum, so I can’t comment on that comparison.

There are two major axis of action in this game, one built around factories, buildings and population, the other one built around shipbuilding, sailing, and colonization. In all likelihood, players will dabble in both areas during the game, but some specialization is lucky, and I suspect true specialization is also possible. Both axis’s take advantage of a two dimensional market across three potential resource types, where colonies or factories are use to generate income, with each one driving down the income possible for their dimension, while increasing the income for the other.

The sailing-shipbuilding-population axis seems to be more action intensive, but has the advantage of giving bonus points, in the forum of explorers, and cash from exploring new territories. The factories-building-population axis tends to be a bit more cash tight but is more action efficient.

Victory points are gained from acquiring prestige markers, which cost population, and increase the player’s personal multiplier for having specific buildings, colonies, or explorers at the end of the game.

Navegador is another game I am currently uncertain about. I generally respect Mac Gerdt’s designs, with an especial appreciation for Imperial/2030 and the Princes of Macchu Pichu, but my one play of this game did not wow me. I expect to get more plays of this in the near future, however, so hopefully it will end up being one that grows on me over time.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Players are Portuguese explorers and businessmen, trying to earn cash and victory points through exploration and exploitation of resources.  Nicely implemented rondel game, using the base mechanic of advancing your pawn around the action track, and taking the action.  Actions include recruiting ships and workers (costing money, but you need them), building buildings (shipyards, churches and goods factories), founding colonies (which provide goods), moving ships (to reach the colonies), purchasing privilege (end game victory point multipliers), and working the market (selling goods, dropping the price, or processing goods, raising the price).  Tightly designed, moments of genuine indecision abound, as everything is worth considering and attractive.  If it has a fault, the game perhaps grinds to a conclusion, forced by one player who has to assume they're winning and take a risk.  Other than that, wonderful - this and Shipyard are the only rondel games you need.

RATING: 8
A weirdly elusive game. I wasn't entirely sure why I lost or won on my several plays - I knew I was outplayed, but why? Bit like life really. I enjoyed my plays of this game, for a euro it's quite thematic and turns are instant (or can be), and with this speed you feel like you've played a weightier longer game in about an hour, which is how fast you can play this game with two. Plays well to very well with two. I like the simplified market, which gives this game a strong economic vibe, tied to an exploration element and a racing aspect. It's not really that reminiscent of anything except games by the same designer, which bodes well. 

There wasn't much I disliked: I wasn't so bothered by the Royal Favour (or whatever) bit, felt fiddly overall and the games certainly thinky over being fun, without much direct influence over other players. Though it isn't solitary as the other players influence is there, though it isn't direct.

Overall I found there was something elusive about this game, like it was trying to show me something just out of reach. It stuck in my mind after playing which is always a good sign, but I'd rather play Concordia. They are not that reminiscent of each other, but there are similarities (the cards in concordia are a sort of rondel) and concordia is more simple, streamlined, plays faster, has a great expansion and is much easier to get to the table. Concordia has worse box art. I quite like the guy on this box, that's how you feel when playing. Your mind is busy, too busy to be having fun? I don't know. Which route do you wish to take, it matters a great deal, but you might not ever realise why.

RATING: 4
This was my first exposure to the rondel.  I liked the rondel mechanism itself, but the rest of the game didn't do much for me, even though on the face of it this game does some things that usually appeal to me, such as market economies.  However, this game has a lot of "bad Euro" characteristics, the most severe of which in my opinion is that there is a lotta math here, and it's bad math because you actually have to work it out turn to turn.  You (or and least I) can't just make an intuitive play and be confident the scoring will reward that choice. 

I also, in principle, like the idea of multiple scoring paths, so that players can do entirely different things and be competitive.  However, here this is done in an unexciting way.  This isn't the first recent Euro in which I felt that everything was oh so carefully balanced that it didn't really matter what I did, as long as I did it efficiently and kept up with the math to make sure to squeeze out every last point.  Yeech.  

Overall, this game was a tedious experience for me and I have little desire to replay it.  The board is gorgeous though.

RATING: N/A
●Essen 2010 release.
●Has Straits of Malacca on the board game map.
●Asked a friend to help me buy, since he is in London. ●Price in London is cheaper than local.

Some comment, didn't cover all elements.
In Navegador, the ONLY luck you can find is where you randomly place the colony tokens on their respective spot across the continent. What you're basically doing is just randomizing the value of the tokens, that's it.

As for the economic, this concentrates on the market. It's also your basic mean of income throughout the game. In the market, players will sell goods (with tokens/plantations) and process goods (with factory). Best thing about this part is, the market prices for each goods (sugar, spices, gold) constantly fluctuate so there can be a little drama there as money is a little scarce in the game as you need it to recruit new workers, build ships, buy colony tokens, and build buildings (there are many of them; church, shipyard and 3 different factories).

Navegador is not a worker placement game, but there's one action where you can recruit new workers. You're just keeping track of the amount of workers that you have (2 to 9), and that's it. You don't actually take your worker and place theme somewhere on the board to take the action, no. In this game, workers are used for you to buy colony tokens, take privilege token, and as a requirement for you to buy/construct buildings.

Is this game intense? Well I'd say yeah. You can't track of someone's VPs while playing, so there's some sense of uncertainty there. You're constantly on the watch as to what people are doing, Individual turn takes one min or two and and there are 7 different actions that a player can do. So it's fast (your next turn takes less than 10 mins?) and games constantly on the move and the pace keep changing.

Comment Update: I absolutely love Navegador with more players, and 5 being my best experience (4 was ok while 3 was not so enjoyable for me). The fact that a player's action only takes mere seconds (that's quick!); thanks to rondel) or 1-2 mins max (I think), make you glued to your seat as the downtime is low. Our 5 players game is always about 105-120 mins max.

There's definitely more tension especially at the market as the price plunge up/down unpredictably (sometimes). Since this game has 5 main ways for you to score VPs (colony tokens, factory, church, shipyard, exploration disc), and with 5 players, you can see how tight the decisions will be.

This is one of my favourite economic game with a nice touch of exploration and management. I absolutely love teaching this game.

RATING: 8.24
Great and fairly complex euro game with ingenious roundel system designed by Mac Gerdts. 
There is a lot of things you can do in 3 game phases: explore seas, found colonies, recruit workers, sell raw goods or manufactured products on the market adjusting the price in two ways, build ships, factories, cathedrals or chase privileges for victory points.
A hidden gem in my collection, especially because I got it as a present.

RATING: 8.5
I like this game, a lot.  It's beautifully produced, thematic, and very strategic.  I think, though, for my tastes, Navegador might be just a little too restrictive.  Like it lacks enough interesting decisions and tension, or something along those lines.

Within a very short period of time, most of the strategies at the table seem to be set, and then it feels like a relatively linear trajectory to the finish.  There certainly are plenty of tactical considerations to be made but they seem smaller, and maybe a bit less impactful than I'd like.

That said, I must still insist that the overall experience of playing is extremely pleasant, enjoyable and something I look forward to.  I can only see the rating moving up as, hopefully, some of the more nuanced elements of the game become more apparent with familiarity.

RATING: 7
A game of multiplayer opportunism; you don't directly interfere with one another but you snipe opportunities and capitalize on/mess with prices in the market. Turns are quick and it plays well with 5P when you want to play an action efficiency game without direct conflict. You're required to manage your infrastructure of buildings and ships while sending your ships out to colonize and grab goods that will make you money via the market action. Specialization is required here (which is against my generalist nature), so I scuffle at the game though I still enjoy it. 

The end is a bit wonky, or so it seems after only a couple plays, as one player will have to take the chance to end the game without full knowledge that they'll win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Navegador is a solid if fairly standard resource optimization game with the rondel being the defining feature.

Most of the victory points come from privileges that act as multipliers for various achievements (e.g. number of colonies established, shipyards built, or regions explored). There are several divergent paths to those achievements, and the major difficulty is aligning those goals with the rondel mechanic. As in most optimization games you never have enough actions to begin with, and you must be careful not to waste some of those precious actions because you're sitting on the wrong side of the rondel. Therefore, looking ahead two to three turns is a good idea especially because many of the actions also need to be paid for, and getting to the desperately needed action space without being able to pay for it is, surprisingly, not going to help much.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
I think Navegador is a fine game. I like how it uses Gerdts' 'Rondel' mechanic in a very easy going way, but with multiple points of tough decision making. On the other hand, there are times where the _best_ move is just glaring, and this may happen one too many times to have this game escape from a rather 'scripted' feel at points. I like the other mechanics- they are very clean, and turns come by quickly. It also feels like a tight, well-balanced game. So, not my favourite in terms of depth, but this is my favourite Gerdts game at this point.

RATING: 6
After three plays -- all wins -- I feel like once one has circled the rondel once or twice, the wheels have been set in motion and players are really just going along for the ride till the end. Perhaps with a table of experienced players, those first few laps round the rondel are tense games of timing. Even still, that leaves the bulk of the game time to just following one's chosen path to victory, because by that point, there's no real changing one's tack.

In my last game, I acted as banker and market adjuster. So I rarely had the opportunity to notice what the other three players were doing on their turns. And yet, in the end, it didn't seem to matter.

RATING: 7
Another rondel game from Mac Gerdts. This time you are sailing the seas, establishing colonies and sell goods. Points are awarded in different things you build or develop.

Generally I think Mac Gerdts’ games are dry optimization games. This is no exception to that, but I like this more than the previous ones and feels a pinch more thematic than Antike and Hamburgum. I have generally hard time for market manipulation in games, but for me this works fine, maybe because it doesn’t feel like you are driving the others to sink. The game features different ways to score points which all seem balanced, but not all the same. It is probably not a game I will pick up, but I will gladly play it if it is suggested.

RATING: 5
I don't agree that it is the best of the rondel games, but it is a solid one. During the game one will specialize in either buildings such as factories, shipyards and churches or colonies, to get most victory points at the end of the game (by getting also the according privilegues). It's just not enough that only half of the game is interesesting.

RATING: 8
Played twice. Starting off at "8" though I expect it to rise. This is another winner from Mac Gerdts, I think the Rondel mechanic is great, I like the way it guides your choices keeping the game going at a good pace. Neat link with theme with clear whole game strategic paths to follow. Do you charge off in your ships on a voyage of discovery, do you take it slower establishing mines in the newly discovered lands (price of goods drops as you flood the market), do you build factories to process materials (uses up raw materials scarcity raises their price) or do you build churches which boosts your population of workers. Fascinating options, to follow more than 2 paths will dilute your endeavour and cause you to lose ground to your opponents. This is my joint favourite Essen 2010 game along with 20th Century. Oct 10

Subsequent outings cemented the early feeling and I think the game works really well, and the theme is an integral part as you do think you are exploring and colonising and using the market wisely to fund these operations. 

One thing that impressed me was there is very little downtime between goes so it moved quickly. Very difficult to determine who is leading so near the end it's a dilemma whether to grab that last navigation counter or building or to have 'just one more' turn on the rondel and let someone else end the game!

Great stuff, a very satisfying 90 minutes.

RATING: 7.5
Fantastic game, quick pace and a fair amount of excitement. The economics of the game are at the core, allowing you to raise the price of goods by producing them with your factories or lowering the price by selling goods for cash. On top of this is an exploration game where you race to explore new sea regions from Portugal to Japan (gaining you colonies that are markets for your sales). Finally, you curry royal favors to maximize the points you get at the end of the game. Game uses a rondel, is NOT a worker placement game, and is thoroughly fun. Highly recommended.

RATING: 7
Another implementation of the rondel, that once again adds a layer of interactivity and phase-timing jokeying, while making turns very short.
More specifically, Navegador mixes a race for discoveries with engine building and market manipulation...  while it does sound complicated, it is not thanks to the rondel.

Nevertheless, Navegador is a top tier game for its great depth/complexity ration and its amazingly short playing time. Too bad the theme is not more present enough.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Navegador is easily the best of Gerdt's rondel games I have played (I have avoided Imperial and Inca Empire somehow so far), and it makes the rondel serve the theme of exploration and trade much better than Antike, and also better than Hamburgum.
Like the other rondel games, the use of the wheel becomes a matter of timing opportunity and aligning that with player strategy, in colony acquisition, factory, shipyard and church building, and in exploration.
For those who like dice, or a little randomness (why are you looking at this designer anyway?) you will not find it here, but likely instead the 'dry, mathematical Euro'. It's not that prototypical Euro exactly, but it's certainly more a model for thoughtful resource acquisition and expenditure than for warfare (even with the natives who were historically colonized.)
There is generally always a decent choice of actions, if not the one you really wanted to take again, and there are definite opprotunities to watch for, but also a variety of methods for collected endgame points.
I found the Market opportunities interesting, the resources as frustrating as usual (Arrgh! I need one more worker to make that Church, but will have to settlte for soemthing else now...), and the overall play moved quickly and logically to a conclusion.
The theme is better tied to the board and the strategy of play than in the other Gerdts' games that I know, and
this made the whole more inviting and interesting, and less of a mathematical exercise in ponderous conquest.
I am starting this at a 7.5 after a 1st solo 3-player game, and will be interested to see how much it comes out. I think this is a good, possibly great game, and I look forward to getting it on the table again soon.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Another Mac Gerdts design, that takes the rondel mechanic and applies it to a "Age of Renaissance" setting, where players send ships west to the Americas first, and then work their way eastward towards the Far East ending in Japan. It is always interesting to see how Gerdts can take the rondel mechanic and revamp it to make a wholly new game with a different theme, and the different feel. While I got crushed in my first game, I can appreciate the design, and look forward to playing it again. However, it may take several more plays to convince me to get it for myself. It's a '7' for now.

Update (05/17/12): It's taken a year to play this again, but learning it anew was a frustrating experience. The typical Euro rules format is wordy, with the font several points too small. The game should be easy to explain, but it's not. The relationships between the actions and turning them into victory points is opaque and convoluted, more so than the linear sequence of Antike and Princes of Machu Picchu. Once this is figured out, the game flows smoothly and quickly. I think this will take a number of plays to play well, much like PoMP. Still a '7'.

RATING: 8.2

規則:
起始設定:
1.市場起始第四格，每個殖民地蓋者洗勻放在對應地點，每個人物放一個對應特權，每塊地點放一個探索標記(除葡萄牙)，工廠教會造船廠由左放到右(人數超過兩人會補不滿。每個最多6間)，尾家拿大航海家卡
**大航海家卡第一輪不能使用,拿到時輪盤上橘色船會到當下行動格標示，若是轉一圈沒使用玩視同放棄。
2.每個人兩艘船在葡萄牙，三個工人在里斯本，200元，一個國王賞賜，個人圖板上有一間萬用工廠，一教堂，一造船廠
3.每輪移動標記在轉輪上1~3格 超過要支付地圖上的船 一格一艘，第一動隨意放
4.船過紅線(好望角、麻六甲)會開始下一個階段，移動點數增加，多人多船變貴，補人物區特權標記
**下一位玩家開始試用新階段花費，若是使用大航海家卡移動，該回合立即生效。
**新階段開始時，依照遊戲版圖說明，按玩家人數多寡補充特權指示物
**遊戲在探索到長崎時結束，或是最後一排的建築物被蓋時進入最一輪，觸發後，每個玩家再一輪，觸發的玩家最後一動後結束
*可做行動如下
A 雇工人 每有一個教堂可花50元雇一個，多雇的要花不同階段的花費，超過上限先付50元買，再用100元賣掉
B 買船 同雇工人只是改看造船廠，船放在葡萄牙，超過上限先付50元買，再用100元賣掉
C 航行 根據現在階段，每艘船移動對應邊界數，有探索標記的要兩艘船進去一艘會爆掉回供應區，拿走探索標記並開殖民地，根據價格最低的獲得金錢，每次只能探索一塊新海域。
D 殖民 滿足條件(一艘船在該區域跟兩個工人可以殖民)，支付一個殖民地的錢拿殖民地版塊，可以一次殖民多區域。
E 建築 工人要足夠，付對應金錢拿建築物
F 市場 殖民地板塊賣看當時時價，賣完下降對應格數，工廠處理看右邊工廠價格，處理幾個上移幾格，可同時賣跟處理不同貨物，同樣的不行買賣一起。
G 特權 減少一個工人拿一個特權(兩個工人時不能做)，並拿該特權上的數字乘上對應項目的金錢獎勵。
特權:殖民地、工廠、探索、教堂、船鎢
*用rondel記分，超過100擺船，主要是個人圖版各項目記分(可用國王特權家加某一欄)，每一個工人跟圖板上的船跟200元一分
＊國王特權（King’s Privilege）之變體規則
  1.當玩家有船停泊在Goa（當時葡萄牙海上帝國的中心），且玩家選擇《特權行動時，玩家可不選擇原本的特權傳奇人物，而選擇行使［國王特權］。
　2.若玩家選擇［國王特權］，玩家不須花費1名工人，而可以直接將《國王特權指示物》放在玩家遊戲板上的空位上，並獲得相應的金錢。
　3.［國王特權］只能行使1次。若玩家於遊戲過程中未能行使［國王特權］，遊戲結束時，該特權作廢。
*海盜與外交官
1.海盜花費一船隻，能力:除了自己的殖民地和工廠外，還可利用每艘船1對1洗劫該區未被擁有的殖命地來賣貨。 使用後將海盜卡翻至背面。最後計分，每艘船多一分，若是有2x探索板塊再加兩分
2.外交官花費一工人，能力: 使用其他1位玩家的殖民地和工廠。 使用後將外交官卡翻至背面。遊戲結束，工人每個多一分。

RATING: 7.5
GCL: http://boardgamegeek.com/listitem/1512386?commentid=1867230#comment1867230
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Fun game about the age of exploration.  All of the players are Portuguese explorers who are trying to collect the most victory points by: exploring unexplored regions, establishing colonies, building factories, shipyards, and churches.  The game board is quite large and illustrated with a nice looking, antique style map of the world.  The art of the map helps the theme come through.  The board also has a circular action selection area called a rondel, which the designer, Mac Gerdtz is famous for.  The rondel serves to make players' turns brief and reduce downtime between turns.  I love the rondel in this game and I also love it in his other game that I have played (Antike) as well.  There is a great market mechanism for the sale and consumption of the three resources in the game: sugar, gold, and spices.  This game has very little player interaction.  I like the mechanism for building, where each church or shipyard or factory costs more than the previously built corresponding building.  I enjoy this game very much.  It has multiple paths to victory and players have stimulating choices to make on their turn.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I did horribly this first game. But hey, the game was great fun and varied. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of my favorite eurogames: despite being relatively easy to learn, it is pretty deep, interactive, fast-paced (no downtime at all) and it has several balanced winning strategies. The market mechanic is superior over the more common market mechanic of Power Grid or Cuba because it is much more dynamic (you can exploit a high price much more by selling several goods for that high price, in contrast to Cuba) and you don't have to refill tokens on the market (more elegant than Power Grid). 

It hits the fine line of being meaty enough for the real geeks and still accessible for the more casual gamers ...

I am surprised why it is not that popular and why I didn't happen to play it earlier ...  

EDIT: One con is that the exploration can actually be neglected in order to win and that the exploration is somewhat linear and boring ...

Well I'm glad I played before I bought. Otherwise I would have wasted my money.

It's a fascinating and very well designed system. It's just not that fun of a game.

In particular, it just felt like you couldn't break out of the mold. I mean sure, you could be a colony whore, or a church whore, but you still needed those dang privileges to win.

And I love the math behind how the market works. I don't like the ~35 second pause everytime someone hit the market to do the math. Just felt clunky compared to the other actions that were lighting fast. (Almost felt like the Maneuver from Antike, but nowhere as near as bad.)

Still, it was much better than Hamburgam or Machu Pichu. There was still some diversification to be had.

Mainly it just fell so flat when contrasted with something like Hansa Teutonica. Each game of Hansa is a book unwritten. Navegador is a novel where you want to read the chapter no-one else is already reading.

RATING: 9
Eigen spel, gespeeld in de Bokkensprong 16/12/'16. Tactisch kolonisatiespel met veel verschillende strategieën. RATING: 8
Cool game. The rondel is a really fun action selection system, and this game in particular makes really good use of it. I really like how you can specialize in certain things and tailor your end game scoring in whatever direction you like. I love how the economy is so symbiotic, you really need to figure out what other people are doing and do something different. If everyone is pushing hard on a certain kind of colony, than the corresponding processing plants will be very valuable. Because of how the map is structured the first person to get around Africa and sell spices will be able to sell them for a lot. The end game triggers are also interesting, and important to be able to predict since they can come up pretty quick after a relatively leisurely mid-game.

RATING: 7
There are good rondel games and there are rondel games which aren’t quite as good.  Happily Navegador  falls into the former category.   The market is the outstanding feature, with competing pressures to push certain goods down in price by selling them, or up by processing them.  There’s also a tension between exploring new regions and colonizing the ones which already exist.

Navegador reminds me most of Hamburgum, which I don’t like as much, but without some of the more irritating aspects of Hamburgum like the race to get buildings first and the pedestrian map.  Navegador is much more thematic, a focus which is welcome.

I’ll play gladly but likely not purchase.

RATING: 8.5
While enjoying Antike and (in particular) Imperial I haven't picked up any of Mac's previous non-conflict games, but this one looked good enough to tempt me.

Good economic/exlporation game based on the age of Portuguese exploration that employs the rondel well. There are usaully many tempting options and you need to be both strategically and tactically flexible.

Strategically you want to avoid ploughing the same furrow as the others, especially those on your right. If they're going for sugar colonies, go for sugar factories or gold colonies; if they're going for shipyards and factories, go for churches and exploration etc.

The value of the market action varies tremendously turn-by-turn based on what the others have done. A strong economic base is very helpful so you need to know when to cash in and when to leave it for a while.

Yet to see a dominant strategy - which is good and suggests the game has legs.

RATING: 8
Good game! This is right up there with Concordia for me (which is quite high) and has further increased my admiration for Mac Gerdts as a designer. The rondel works excellently, and the Navegador bonus tile that moves around in reverse turn order is a nice feature as well.

In my opinion, the real star of this game is the market with the changing prices for producing vs. processing the resources. It makes for some interesting decisions as you try to choose which resources to focus on based on the plantations and processing buildings other people are using. Love this game!

After playing the Salsa expansion for Concordia, I don't think I would ever choose Navegador over it. Still it is quite good!

RATING: 6
Tentative rating after one play.  I like rondel games and this one seems to have some potential.  I also like the way the market works.  But its also got a lot of gamey-ness.  For example, the way that different phases of the game happen when ships pass a certain point.  I just don't like that - seems artificial.  Worse is the way the cost of buildings increase exponentially - its an obvious heavy-handed catch-up mechanism.  Compare this with Dominion (for example), where the catch-up mechanism is that as your deck fills up with VP cards it becomes harder to get more.  That's elegant.  Navegador is not.  Its like after some playtesting someone decided that there was a bit too much runaway leader so they went for a simple 'fix' of escalating prices.  Boring.  The game keeps reminding you that you are only playing a game and not sailing the oceans.  MacGerdts has excellent ideas but he should get a new developer.

RATING: 8
Another good implementation of the rondel mechanism. Even the investor card from Imperial shows up in a new reiteration as the navegador card giving additional movement this time. Not as good as Imperial but way better than Antike. 
At its heart, Navegador is a classic eurogame of building up your engine of gathering resources, converting them to money and finally to VP's the most efficient. It pays out to focus on one area of development but it's mandatory to establish colonies and/or factories in order to generate cash. The varying values of the secret discovery tokens introduce a slight luck factor. This can screw one unsuspecting player early on if he discovers a low-priced colony and the player with the Navegador card buys it. The market for selling or manufacturing goods could have been more interesting. As it is, the decision on selling or manufacturing is obvious in that you don't want to leave high price opportunities for other players. 
The jury's out if this game is needed considering that it is at least the fourth incarnation of the rondel mechanism. I have to play Hamburgum to find out.

RATING: 8
Like everyone else on BGG says, the rondel seems to be reaching a saturation point, but Navegador really does improve on all of the previous rondel games (with the possible exception of Imperial, which I have not yet explored enough to rate with full confidence).  The length is just about right and there really are multiple viable paths to victory.

Probably the only criticism I have is a very minor one, since it pertains to a lot of games.  You are definitely constrained by the player order, in that you have to choose your path based on what the other players have already chosen.  It would be really hard to win doing the exact same thing that another player is doing - you need to react and hopefully counteract what another player is doing.  I really like the market and factory and how they work together.  It is fun to have that work to your advantage, much to the other players' consternation.

Again, a very ho-hum theme, but you can't have it all, I guess.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This game is a true work of art: everything is intertwined, streamlined, fat free. Despite that, the game has some amazing mechanisms: the rondel, which is ordered in such a way that you can't optimize all of your turns; the scoring by multiplication, which is also very competitive to get tokens for; the market, which self-balances and forces you to choose between a balanced economy, which assures you a consistent income, or a specialized one, which means your Market phase will pay a lot, but you won't decide when to do them, but you'll be at the market's will.

Plus, it has a really quick pace, turns take under a minute, and so downtime is consistently low.

RATING: 6
En economic model and engine game with an almost linear exploration track. The significance of actions is initially opaque, but success seems to boil down to: a) specialization, b) doing what no one else is doing, and c) most particularly not doing what the player on your left is doing. Three may not be the ideal player count, since there are only two "engines" and whoever doesn't have to compete should benefit greatly. Slightly mathy market operations. Quite attractive overall.

Update: Fun also with four, but the game ends very quickly after you reach the spice colonies.

RATING: 9
What is interesting in Navegador is not carrying out a strategy, but in the early game deciding what strategy to go for. Each turn will change which is the best strategy to pursue and you must be flexible and study the game state well. 

As the game goes into the middle and end you must run your strategy (the easy part), while timing all your moves to best take advantage of when is best to do things like privilege, sailing, markets, or anything really, because since you have to do it all (or just about) choosing when to do one thing affects when you do the more contested things. 

Excellent game that I always plan to play. A true test of skill and something that will not get old to play if all the players understand where the heart of the game lies.

RATING: 7
A solid euro that's fairly unforgiving & where newbies' mistake will benefit one, but not all of the other players.  For me, it suffers from the same combo I do not like in Shadow of the Emperor: Hidden scores + Players fighting for points from a common pool.  You take points from other players (colonizing where they could have, grabbing the multiplier they also needed, taking the buildings that are worth a lot to them, etc.) all the time.  Since the scores are just totaled at the end, it's easy to take from the wrong person and fail at balancing the scores.  At any given point, doing the right thing would really involve taking out pen and paper and counting how many points each player has, which is totally viable since all that info is public, but completely impractical.  So games end up being won by a handful of points which could have gone to someone else but didn't because bothering to get accurate information to decide your play is enough tedium to make the game not fun.  There's a lot to like in there, but that kills it for me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
LIKE:

* There appears to be several different paths to victory available in the game.
* Your plan needs to reflect what other people are doing, particularly those of your righthand neighbor!  You do not want to get stuck trying to do the same thing as him/her.
* Rondel appears well implemented in this game.  I felt like there was a viable and valuable option almost the whole game no matter where I was positioned on the rondel.
* Stock market mechanic is nicely implemented.  I could see where this area could be manipulated to some degree at a cut-throat level.  

DISLIKE:

* While I see the value of the Navegador tile (needed to counteract early player advantage to some degree), I am not sure how much I like it in play the whole game.  There is potential for it to mess up well laid plans depending on how and WHEN people choose to use the tile and it's ability.
* Not sure how I feel about the randomness of the distribution of the plantation tiles.  I understand the desire to provide and explore concept to the game and the uncertainty that comes with exploring, but every time I explored (4 times), I turned over high cost plantations.  Blargh!

In the end, I think this is one of those games for which I'd love to trade BUT not so much buy.

RATING: N/A
Arrived
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
We ended up getting this from our Secret Santa and finally got it to the table.  With the two of us, we used Tom Lehmann's suggested variant (found [thread=671709]here[/thread]), which seemed to work quite well.  It took us about half of a game to really pick up on what was going on---what's nice is that there are really only seven actions that you have to know.  Even then, your available actions are generally constrained by the rondel to the next three spots.  This makes each turn relatively quick and helps to cut down on analysis paralysis.  However, although the actual actions are relatively easy to learn, it does take a little bit to pick up on how each action affects the others.  It's a remarkably subtle game---each action is tightly intertwined with the others.  Nowhere is this more apparent than in the market, where the prices of the three resources (sugar, gold, and spice) are explicitly affected by selling from colonies and then processing using factories.  This makes the game feel very dynamic---it's not just about sailing and exploring, but there's this engine underneath the game that you have to understand.

We found that with two players, the game has a very pleasant feel.  Generally, we both end up focusing on several of the five scoring categories, but even with two you still have to keep an eye out on what your opponent is doing.  I can see how with more players, the gameplay might be even more dynamic---for example, with more players, the market would fluctuate much more.  Still, we enjoyed it as a two player game (although I do wonder about replayability over the long haul, since there isn't too much randomness to the game---will we end up settling for familiar strategies?)

RATING: 8
I'm totally uncertain about how Mr. Gherdts does it. It's yet a totally new take on using the rondel, or at least building a game around it. 

It's essentially set collection and each set on your personal tableau gives additive bonuses to the sets of buildings and colony tiles you collect. The buildings also expand other abilities on the rondel as your sets of them burgeon in size.

The sugar, gold, and spice "shacks" are used in conjunction with the colonies to either sell good and devalue the market or the shacks serve to revalue it; either way you can make great money and careful diversification is necessary. 

I was hesitant and perhaps future plays will show it has some sort of limited staying power but the game was competitive and tense throughout, all players saw the other forks in the road they could have taken, and in all everyone agree about how good it was.

It's easily the second best in the series but could just as easily grow to be my favorite.
