RATING: 8.5
Based on two plays I think this is one of the better rondel games from Mac Gerdts. I am really fond of the rondel mechanism since it makes the game flow quickly even with 5 players. 
This is an intense game loaded with strategy, interaction and different paths to victory. Even tho it feels like a game where you have to explore the seas in the first play there soon relveals several alternatives for gaining victory points. 
It is a game deep into the Euro category and no combat what so ever all interaction are at competition and economic level. Great game with seemingly lots of replayability.

RATING: 7
This is a solid game. The rondel mechanism means there is a cost to playing a focussed strategy that makes for interesting decisions, the market fluctuations place an emphasis on timing, and competition for limited resources makes this game more directly competitive than is common in Euros. However, the theme is uninspired (even if it is well-researched), and the artwork, though attractive, is also somewhat bland. These factors don't reduce my enjoyment of the game in any way, but they do cause it to get overlooked when choosing what game to play, and consequently this game does not see the frequency of play that it ought to.

RATING: 8.4
First impression after 2 x 4p games online.  This is an almost zero luck strategic game.  The rules overhead is a little more than I usually like and the choices are not as intuitive as I would like.  However, this game is very interesting and exciting.  Good degree of interaction for a Euro.  It's not MPS -- although you don't feel like you can directly affect a player your strategy and actions affect all players.  Definitely lots of right/left binding that make the game in part group dependent.  Learning curve.  Beautiful components/map although not easy to decipher until one has some practice playing the game.  This game put Mac Gerdts on my radar -- I had heard many good things about his designs and this corroborates those positive reviews.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of Gerdts' strongest rondel offerings.  The commodity market which has players driving prices up and down works much better than the markets we see in other games.  You really need to pay attention to player order (the player in front of you) to choose a feasible market strategy.  The great thing about this design though is that you don't even really need to invest too heavily in the market to compete.

The market has 3 goods (sugar, gold & spices).  Players gain these goods by moving their ships around the board and purchasing plantations on colonies.  Ships that first enter a new territory receive extra income and a navigator token.  

For each plantation you own you can sell the good it produces by using the Market action.  This moves the prices down in the market.  Players can also invest in factories which process goods and moves the prices of those goods up in the market.  You just cannot sell and process the same good during your market action.

There are a number of ways to score points and generally speaking you want to pick one or two strategies and then collect privileges for those strategies.  Privileges act as bonus VP multipliers at the end of the game.  You can score in Churches (that provide you with cheaper workers), Shipyards (that provide you with cheaper ships), Colonies, Factories, Money, Ships, Workers (that allow you to take privileges, colonize and purchase buildings) & Exploration tokens.

Although my first impression was very strong, my major concern, if it comes to pass, is that it seems one wants to choose a strategy that fits with what other players are doing (as you don't want to compete directly with too many players). Sticking with that strategy your moves around the rondel would be largely programmed.  So I'm not too sure about this game's long-term replay value.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Still thinking about this one. Heavily dependent on turn order, and also the pressure of the rondel and handover of the Navegador card, so it can feel like you are dealing with very prescripted actions where the exact order and timing in relation to other players matters a lot. Takes a bit of time to grasp the market interactions: colonies flood the market with goods (driving prices down for raw goods, but making factories that rely on raw goods more profitable); factories flood the market with produced goods (driving prices down for more products but up for more raw goods from the colonies). The main tip for newbies would be: don't get a factory and colony of the same type (since you are not allowed to both produce AND sell the same type of good in a single market action, so one of your investments will go to waste), and try to get the opposite of what other players are investing in, especially if they are earlier in turn order (so if they get a gold colony, either get a sugar colony or a gold factory, so you get better prices in the market). -- It is an elegant system, as you'd expect from Gerdts, and has just enough randomness to avoid repetitive play (as colonies vary wildly in value). Players will do best if they figure out their role in the game ecosystem and avoid competing directly on the same turf as opponents--if one player buys up lots of factories, go into colonies or exploration, or buy different types of factories. Snag privileges whenever you can without fatally depleting your workforce.

Major issue I can see is that there's no timer to the game beyond people scooping up buildings or exploring to the end of the board. Unlike Concordia, which flat out gives you 7 VPs for triggering endgame, the reward for doing so here is subtler and more situational: you get the last move, which will presumably mean you bought the last building (and thus can if nothing else go to market for a shower of money for points on the last move) or picked up the last explorer token (and thus can buy up the last few colonies or go to market on the last move). 

Playing Navegador online at Yucata means that it's always visible who's in the lead--and this is a game where the gap between players simply widens as the game goes on. There is no catch-up mechanic to boost a second-rate engine. Playing in real life probably obscures that fact a lot more. I like knowing exactly where I stand in a game at all times, but your tastes may vary.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This is a fun enough game, but it really does seem as though all of the interesting decisions are made in the first three or four turns. Once the players have collectively worked out their positioning / phasing among the main strategic tacks, the only real thing left to do is let the game run its course. Most of the player decision-making from that point on is fairly obvious, and the only people with tougher decisions to make are those who are in bad positions and probably won't be able to recover anyway. There are a couple of minor timing decisions, but basically your early game decisions are going to put you into a fairly straight-forward 3- or 4-stop path around the rondel that you really don't veer very far from.

RATING: 8
I just played this game for the first time and was impressed. There are no dice or cards involved so luck plays little in the game play. The only luck factor is in the random way the colonies are distributed on the map.

The rondel mechanism is an elegant way for players to decide what actions to execute. The comodities market is an interesting take on supply/demand economics.

There are multiple strategies that a player can employ while playing this game. In my game there were 4 players.  The winner focussed on generating enough money to build churches, which he used to obtain cheaper workers, which he used to obtain privilege multipliers on churches. At the end he racked 54 points (6 churches x 9). I took the more traditional route of building ships, exploring and colonizing. It was good enough for 2nd place. Another player monopolized the gold market by building all the gold factories. This strategy did expose one flaw in the game; there was not enough money provided. We had to use 1000 coin token (from another game) to give the gold baron. 

I can not wait for the next chance to play this game.

RATING: 8
In this Rondel game of 16th cenury Portugal, one is taking advantage of the sugar, gold and spice trade to make money, while trying to increase their workers and buildings, while exploring further in the world. 

This is the best game of the Rondel series. You have to be very precise in the opening moves, and after that there are different strategies to pursue. There is a "Navegador" mechanic where the last turn player gets to sail for free, then passes on the Naegador ability to balance the first turn player advantage.

There are a lot of things to think about and each game is tense and exciting. I'm looking forward to more plays.

RATING: N/A
2/11,
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.25
心得:
還不錯玩的遊戲
代入感不錯
且遊戲節奏控制有點神奇
規則:
*市場起始第四格，每個殖民地蓋者洗勻放在對應地點，每個人物放一個對應特權，每塊地點放一個探索標記(除葡萄牙)工廠教會造船廠有左放到右(人數超過兩人會補不滿)，尾家拿航海卡
*每個人兩艘船在葡萄牙，三個工人在里斯本，200元，一個國王賞賜，個人圖板上有一間萬用工廠，一教堂，一造船廠
*每輪移動標記在轉輪上1~3格 超過要支付地圖上的船 一格一艘，第一動隨意放
*船過紅線會開始下一個階段，移動點數增加，多人多船變貴，補人物區特權標記
*遊戲在探索到長崎時結束，或是最後一排的建築物被蓋時進入最一輪，觸發的玩家最後一動後結束
*可做行動如下
A 雇工人 每有一個教會可花50元雇一個，多雇的要花不同階段的花費，顧超過上限可用100元賣掉
B 買船  同雇工人只是改看造船廠，船放在葡萄牙
C 航行 根據現在階段，每艘船移動對應邊界數，有探索標記的要兩艘船進去便且一艘回手，拿走探索標記並開殖民地，根據價格最低的獲得金錢，每次只能探索一塊地
D 殖民 每一艘船跟兩個工人可以支付一個殖民地的錢拿殖民地版塊
E 建築 工人要足夠，付對應金錢拿建築物
F 市場 殖民地板塊賣看當時時價，賣完下降對應格數，工廠處理看右邊工廠價格，處理幾個上移幾格，可同是賣跟處理不同貨物，同樣的不行
G 特權 減少一個工人拿一個特權(兩個工人時不能做)，並拿蓋的數字乘上對應項目的金錢補償
*航海卡在第一輪不能使用，之後使用往右傳，拿到時放橘船在該玩家所以輪盤格上，如果繞一圈沒用就右傳
*用rondel記分，超過100擺船，主要是個人圖版各項目記分(可用國王特權家在某一欄)，每一個工人圖板上的船跟200元一分

RATING: 9
領航者
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A rondel game with seemingly multiple strategic options rather than the normal mini-races-but-all-doing-the-same-thing feel that rondel games usually provide. This is because you get to "buy" the victory point multipliers in the investment types (colonies, factories, explorations, shipyards, churches/workers) that you wish to specialise in. The initial game is about getting your money engine going first, and then players start to diverge along different investment types. From then on, there's a continual question of how often do you allow yourself to get "distracted" doing turns that allow you to get victory points that aren't in your main specialisation, 'cause that could get you or cost you the game. Turns go pretty fast. I like the new market mechanic which moves prices either way depending on how you sell. There's no gripping tension, and no direct pussage. It simply provides the challenge of organising yourself over the long haul, over many, many actions, as best you can. Which can start getting a bit tiresome upon replay, going (in your best Murray Walker voice) round and round and round ... doing much the same things. I prefer it with more players because you're more racing against other players to get that next shipyard / church / colony before them, eyeing off where they're up to to help you decide what to do next (ie how fast should you circle the rondel). With fewer players it feels more like you're fighting the rondel for efficiency rather than the other players with each player specialising in their own strategy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is the first Mac Gerdts "rondel" game that I've really liked.  Players use their boat tokens to explore the world, from South America and Africa all the way out to Japan.  While exploration is a good source of points, you can also set up colonies on the various explored regions to produce raw materials, or buy factories to process raw materials.  The game features and supply-and-demand track, in which prices go up if raw materials are produced and go down if they're processed.  The game encourages you to specialize, but you can't do so too much because of the rondel, and the more people following the same strategy, the worse that strategy will be.  The game ties together very well despite everything that's going on.  I really enjoyed this.

(5+ plays)

RATING: 8.5
Very enjoyable game with an engaging theme. Strategies seem very balanced and the decision each turn as to which action to select is always interesting. Mac Gerdts gets criticised for his love of the rondel mechanism but it works very well here (as in most of the games where he uses it, to be honest) and there's great tension between wanting to advance slowly and take every action or rush round to take one or two actions more often. This is a really good and somewhat underrated game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent rondel game. The rondel makes actions limited, short and sweet, but also adds a lot of strategic considerations. It makes it so there's always something you can do to further your own position, but the player who can most easily skip spaces and race around the rondel is in a very good position.

What sets this game apart from others is that all players will have different strategies. One player will explore, build wharfs and ships, while another is happy sailing after them and pick up the colonies, and a third doesn't sail at all and builds factories and churches. In fact, not doing what others are doing is the way to success. You can't plan your strategy in advance, but have to adapt to what the other players are (not) doing. 

It's ultimately an efficiency game. You want to focus as much as possible, don't waste actions, and have enough income that you can spend your actions on points instead of money. There is a lot of player interaction, but if you can avoid interacting with your opponents that's probably a good idea. So it's all fairly linear, once everyone has figured out what they want to be doing. But if you like managing your actions, resources and future options, you'll like Navegador.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I think I found the second play with 5 more interesting than with 2 because of the competition for the resources, so I had to focus on the profitable gaps i.e. the often mentioned comment of doing something no-one else is doing.

So instead of getting involved in the sailing on the crowded waters, the lottery of exploring and the colonising (which became expensive) I piggybacked on the market fluctuations with my meagre collection of colonies and factories whilst finding ways of generating income as the pressure of buying shipyards and churches increased. And I was surprised to find I was only 4 vps behind the winner.

Could it be a design to counter groupthink, I don't know and unfortunately it isn't a design I wanted to reflect and try to improve on because despite the interesting challenge of worming through to victory as well as the enjoyable, frustrating sequencing of the rondel, the scoring is too fractured and the multiplying nature of the vps makes the status of players opaque so no strong sense of the race, and with no hidden elements there is no exciting reveal.

In summary, the navigation is spacially the same and the experience dry.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After two plays, this is a contender for best game of 2010. Second only to Troyes, Navegador is, as best I can describe it, Puerto Rico grafted onto the Rondel system. 

Interactive in the same way as PR--watch your opponents, craft your strategy so as best to take advantage of their predictable actions, try to do something different from the player on your right.

Strategic, yet with tactical decisions.

Multiple, clearly defined strategies.

Left-right binding.

Early moves somewhat scripted based on a player's table position and what the other players do however, not as much as PR is scripted. Newbies who make too many mistakes early on may never be able to recover.

All of this adds up to a game where every move is important. Everything you do must be part of your goal, and deviation from your strategy can be very difficult to recover from. I like this kind of punishing aspect of games like PR, Age of Steam, and for me Navegador fits in quite nicely.

I am only hesitant to rate this higher initially because it does not look like it would scale well to 2 players. Like many of Gerdts' games, the zero-sum aspect makes the 2p experience a different beast and not something I enjoy in games of this type because it leads to too much calculation and downtime.

However, I do expect with more plays this could easily be a 9.

RATING: 6
In this game, players use a roundel to take actions which involves sailing your ships around, hiring workers, constructing buildings and taking score multipliers to your board. The look of the game is nice, it has that classic euro game appearance. The rules are easy enough to follow. 

The game feels a bit long for the experience it delivers despite being relatively short. I'm sort of tired with the action options somewhere at the midpoint of the game as it has a lather rinse repeat feel. There are a few paths you can follow, but they're more or less equivalent to each other in terms of how they translate to endgame vp, and the feel of one road vs another isn't different. The game also offers very little variation game to game in terms of setup. There's nothing particularly bad or wrong with this game, it's just not very interesting or remarkable.

RATING: 8
A lot of really cool mechanics, but not all strategies are equally viable. I wish exploration was a bit more key - I don't like that the game encourages slow exploration. This causes newbies to rush to Nagasaki to their detriment, since they tend to associate end-game state with 'good strategy.' I feel like this ought to be an equally viable strategy, if only so that you don't miss the whole point on the first game.

There are two flaws I see. First, the gold resource is too end-game heavy, so one of the resources just doesn't have the same utility. The other is that it just doesn't behoove you to try and get on both sides of the same resource supply - you're better off heavily investing in one direction and getting a higher multiplier of a low number than you are in diversifying on the same resource and making money either way it swings. No supply chain or vertical integration.

RATING: 5
I really like Mac Gerdts' designs because they stand out a bit from the samey JASE crowd. I like how fast it moves with only one action per turn and usually only three to choose between. Interest reduced after 2nd play - strategic paths seem too confining and player who is left alone will likely win.

RATING: 3
= 2013-03-29 - R:NONE

Tried to play through once a couple weeks ago. It was late so we only got part way through (NOTE: it's also not a good sign that bedtime has a stronger draw than the game).

In short, it was boring. I admit that 2p is a poor number for this game and because of that I'm going to refrain from giving the game a low rating, but I also feel that there just wasn't much to offer here. There's stuff you can do. That stuff gives you points. Most points wins. I've heard there are two single-line strategies for this game: (1) don't do what anyone else is doing and (2) benefit from the person to your right and screw the person to your left. Again, this makes it a poor game for 2p.

There was a thread on BGG recently that talked about why people rate games with luck lower and one comment was that some players like to assert mental superiority over others. That's the game. "I'm better at filling out this spreadsheet than you are. I win". We keep running into games like that and Navegador may fall into that category. Sure, you need to pay close attention to what the other person is doing, and there are ways to cause the player to your left some grief, but the grief is mostly just in not letting that person get quite as many points on this turn.

I think it's an excellent game. I'd give it a 7 or 8 if this was the kind of game we liked; however, we don't like this sort of game and it's extra boring for 2. Going on the for trade list.

= 2013-05-09 - R:3

Gave this a rating and traded it away. I've been complaining for a while that BGG favors boring, low-interaction, spreadsheet games. In the case of Glen More and Navegador, I acknowledged that they were well put together spreadsheets and if I liked that sort of game, I might give it a high rating. In both cases I then stated it wasn't a game for me and abstained from giving a rating at all. Today I decided I'm not going to do that any more. No more leniency for boring spreadsheets pretending to be games. This "game" sucked. Perhaps my poor experience with this byzantine abacus will remind me to ignore the BGG ratings in the future.

RATING: 6
Solid economic Euro game. I really like the market mechanism with the price (or rather the profit) for raw materials and processed goods. 

Unfortunately I am no fan of the core mechanism: Mac Gerdt's rondell for action selection. I think this is one of the most unthematic mechanisms. At first glance it might represent something like time and that you can perform a certain action only  once in a certain time frame. But then time is different for all players. My colonies only produce if I spent an action (time) for that. So this does  not really feel like an economy, which would give an automatic income stream. Interestingly, I had  the same feeling when playing Gerdt's new game Concordia, which substitutes the rondell with a deck of cards for limiting your action selection.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Big Mac brings the rondel to a game about Portugese maritime exploration. Actions: 1) Sailing your ships -- the number of spaces moved changes by phase as the game progresses, and there is a natural attrition of one ship lost per fleet each time you enter uncharted waters, both great touches and in synch with theme; 2) Purchasing discovered colonies, allows you to sell goods for gold; 3) Buying workers (one of several forms of currency in the game); 4) using the Market to Produce goods from your factories or Sell goods for money (producing and selling essentially allow market price manipulation in one direction or the other; there are two of these spots on the rondel, so owning a number of Sugar factories and Sugar colonies could get you a nice money generating cycle); 5) Buying ships (whuch always start in Portugal); 6) Buying factories, churches, and shipyards (for resource processing, worker purchasing, and ship placement ostensibly, but more importantly act as huge VP generators at game end); and 7) Buying Priveleges -- there are limited ones available, but they act as multipliers for endgame scoring. Taking more than 3 steps on the rondel is more costly than in past games (you must remove a ship from the board) so mostly done near the end of the game. An optimization game with difficult decisions and a great deal to track. My favorite of the rondel games I've played from MG so far (Imperial, Hamburgum, Navegador, Princes).

RATING: 7.6
I enjoyed this game. It has a simple circular trade (turn money into resources which you use to acquire other types of resources which you use to generate money) which works very well and is quite challenging. The trick is that everything in the game is worth victory points but not everything is worth equal amounts of victory points and you need over the course of the game to maximise your investment in the things that score the most victory points.
It is challenging without being overly competitive and relatively easy to pick up. The arithmetic on the market place is challenging by Euro-game standards (addition of three effectively 2 digit numbers) but I actually like that the game doesn't insult your intelligence in that regard.

RATING: 8
I enjoyed this Mac Gerdts rondel game. I came to it after Concordia, and Navegador does feel like Concordia's father.

I think Concordia is the better game--it's less mathy and you can customize your play style by buying cards.  There's also a bit more direct player interaction by building, making it more expensive for other players to build where you already are.  

That said, Navegador neatly simulates an economy and is fun in its own way.  Navegador is more about trying to capitalize on doing what other players aren't doing, whereas Concordia is more about running your own strategy as efficiently as possible.

In the end though, I felt that there wasn't enough room in the collection for both. Whenever I wanted to play this style of game, I always preferred to play Concordia.

RATING: 8
A very good game with the rondel once again providing a charming simplicity on the gameplay side, but choices galore when it comes to your strategy.

There's a lot of tactical reacting to the actions your opponents perform here, and I appreciate that you can usually see it coming a turn or two off and try to plan to take advantage of their moves. At the same time you're trying to think three or four moves ahead to keep your momentum going, so it's a good game that has you moving around a preplanned path torn between breaking off your preordained plan when opportunity comes knocking or resisting temptation.

There's good balance here, so you can try different strategies out game to game and enjoy the replay value out of that. 

Of the Gerdts Rondel games I still prefer Imperial and Imperial 2030, but this one is a completely different beast and comes in close behind. Navegador will probably be better accepted by gaming groups than the Imperial games (I've unfortunately experienced a dud Imperial game, plus there's the whole Investor card or no Investor card debate you have to agree to), and because it scales well from 2-5, and boy is there a lot to take in when you're playing with five. I see it getting a lot of play. One of the best games from 2010.

RATING: 1
Rating after 1 solo play. I know how it sounds, but I'm unlikely to ever play it "for real". Problem is, I've tried this after a session of Imperial which intrigued me and really stuck in my memory. And this ... well, it's nothing like it. 

[u]Comparing Navegador to Imperial[/u] - these games might not even belong in same hobby, let alone genre. The rondel system on its own is a clever way of pulling levers that pushes you in some kind of sequence. So to be good at a rondel game one needs some practice in order to get a handle on the levers. But there's a huge difference: in Navegador you're pulling levers on your own, as if you would try to find a way to optimise ladders to reach a particular space in an empty tower (okay, weird metaphor, stay with me here). Sure others will take particular  ladders and those won't be there for you, but that's the whole interaction - running for these ladders. In Imperial, you're all in the same space and everyone's in zero gravity, so what ever move you make (what ever lever you pull), you're moving everybody, the whole system. Imperial thus takes a while to learn as everyone will try to adopt and roll with these shifts. Navegador on the other hand doesn't require such skill, it's more about getting your head into the spread sheet that's so neatly drawn on the board. 
[u]Imperial is a game, Navegador is a puzzle. [/u]

In the end of the day Navegador feels to me a bit like Puerto Rico (turn order effect), a bit of Goa (scoring spread sheet) and a bit of JASE's spread sheet logic, so if I ever feel like playing something in this vain, I'll probably pick PR or PoF. And what's with the spice trading shtick? Didn't work any better in Goa... Is there a spice trading game that feels thematically immersive?

But if Gerts wants to do this sort of games all the time in the future, I don't mind, Imperial makes up for everything else he made or might made. For those people who find Imperial hard to digest (Eric Summerer, ahem), there are plenty other rondel games to choose. And if you're one of them and  like this, well, more power to you. 

Rating down to 1.0 because: uninspired AND brown. Without one of these it might still be 1.5, but...

A szerző újabb rondellás játéka, amire ugyanazt tudom írni, mint az első ilyen játékára az Antike-ra. Teljesen korrekt darab, de nem nyűgöz le. Teljesen más a téma, a játékosok felfedezők szerepébe bújnak és gyarmatokat hódítanak meg, begyűjtve az értékes arany, cukor és fűszer terményeket. A játékban a szerencse szerepe nem túl magas, az egyes gyarmatok feldezésénél jelentkezik, ki mennyi pénzt tud ekkor begyűjteni. A pontozás nagyon jól kitalált és elég kiegyenlített végeredmények tudnak lenni, ami pont az általános gondom a rondellás játékokkal. Számomra teljesen egyértelmű, hogy a 3 ingyenes lehetőségből melyik a legjobb, úgy érzem sok gondolkodást nem igényel ez a döntés, pedig ez a fő döntés tulajdonképpen. Ebben a játékban még könnyebb is ez, mert a további lépések ára szerintem ebben a játékban a legfájóbb így nagyon ritkán teszik meg a játékosok. Értékes felfedezőhajót kell adni, minden egyes plusz lépésért. Én úgy gondolom, hogy egy rondellás játék bőven elég egy gyűjteménybe, mert hiába teljesen mások a témák, én a főmechanizmus miatt nem érzek jelentős különbségeket játékélmény szempontjából ezeknél a játékoknál. Nekem még mindig az Imperial tetszik a legjobban, de szívesen kipróbálom majd a Hamburgumot is.

RATING: 8
Feels a bit like Hamburgum but more refined (and, of course, with a different theme) and also with a twist on Imperial's investor cards. May be the best of his lighter rondel games, maybe as good as Imperial?

Players are Portuguese explorers exploring, colonizing, selling/refining goods, and building buildings. It's all driven by the rondel, where you can choose actions to build ships, move ships (explore; earn cash as you do so), colonize, build buildings (churches, shipyards, factories for the 3 types of goods), recruit workers (cost money; spend them to colonize; need minimum number to build various buildings), go to market (to sell/process goods, earn cash), or buy privileges (end game VP multipliers based on how well you've done all those other things). 

As with most rondel games, you want to do a bunch of things but must choose one; generally, if you can make a plan over several turns, you take incremental steps each turn and the game moves along briskly. But you always want to do more than one thing, so you end up reassessing every turn (hopefully, quickly!). 

Works very well! One possible downside that it shares with Puerto Rico -- your options depend on what the player before you does (you don't want to do the same thing!). Might that be a problem if playing with mixed-experience groups?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

Disclaimer #2: Navegador was also designed because of me (I say it with no bit of modesty, I admit).

Played it with 2 and 3 players. Still finding the right number but I believe that 4 players should be it.
Components are a blast and that board is awesome!

RATING: 8
I am a big Rondel games fan.  I really like the rondel.  I loved it in Imperial and Imperial 2030, but in those it can get a little repetitive.  Here, it offers lots of juicy choices and based on your strategy, you can skip some actions for almost the entire game.    In Navegador, you have ample strategies, with a similar numbers of actions on the rondel.  Each strategy has the same basic building blocks (the most important of these is cash), but from there you can pursue one of the basic strategies and ride it to victory.   Here is a brief overview.  Each player is an explorer/entrepreneur in colonial era  Portugal.   You start with workers and ships.  Workers are need to build factories, colonies, shipyards and churches.  Factories and colonies generate cash, Shipyards allow you to build ships cheaply, churches allow you to produce workers cheaply.  Each of these is also worth VP's.  On each players card, you will track your assets and a multiplier for each asset.  At the end of the game you multiply the numbers of an asset times the multiplier for VP's.  For example, if you have 6 colonies, and your colony multiplier is 4, you would have 24 VP's at end game scoring.

The market action is the key to income generation.  Colonies generate either sugar, gold or spice and are sold to the market.  the market gets driven down as goods are sold to it.  Factories (which are purchased by type), use goods and generate a profit based on the market price.   Factories are "using" the goods and hence raise the market.  the profit from factories gets better as the goods get cheaper, so it is in the players best interest to buy factories of goods that are being sold to the market frequently.  

To throw another factor in the equation, this is at its heart an exploration game.  You have ships which progress along the board exploring new areas gaining exploration tokens (which generate valuable VP's since you start with a x4 multiplier), and you get to discover native colonies.  When an area is explored, a stack of colony markers are flipped over revealing a cost.  These costs are varied and represent the cost to develop the colony.  the person who discovered gets cash based on the lowest value.   Later, an action can be taken to colonize.    So exploring gains you VP's and generally gives you first crack at developing a colony.  

So explore, build shipyards, build churches, build factories or develop colonies.  Each seems viable for winning, each seems almost necessary for moving forward.  An interesting mix of development vs, VP generation.  I have only played once but I can see this taking many, many plays before I figure out what strategy is best.  You see, since everything generated VPs, it is not like most games of this type where you go for optimal early game development and late game VP generation.  Almost every action contributes to both.  

A solid game that deserves more play.

RATING: 8
After my first play of the uber-boring Hamburgum, I wasn't sure I wanted to revisit the rondel.  Boy, am I glad I did.  Navegador takes a great mechanism and builds a superb game around it.  What's particularly interesting about Navegador is that every single action is useful at almost every time.  What you end up doing is working to make sure you maximize every one of those actions.

The market in the game is pure awesome.  I love how as more stuff gets sold the price drops, but that incents people to produce the product instead of selling it, thus driving the cost back up.  It's really quite awesome and is enough to make the game great by itself.

I'll rate this one an 8 to start.  I hope I get to play this one more because it really could climb quite a bit.

RATING: 9
Superb rondel-driven exploration game. The aim is to increase your colonies and the workers, factories, churches and shipyards within them, which both aid your development and count towards victory points. As in all rondel games you have to determine which choices to pass over and how to develop the balanced strategy needed to be successful i.e. there is no point building ships if you don't have the necessary currency and workers available to colonise. In several previous releases ('Antike', 'Imperial', 'Hamburgum' etc) designer Mac Gerdts has proven that he can combine the rondel as a driving mechanism excellently with games that provided numerous strategic challenges, and each new publication feels quite differently from the last (no simple rehashing of old ideas here). For me though, 'Navegador' is the pinnacle of his rondel series of games so far.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Ratings (1 to 5):

Simplicity: 5
--> Minimal rulebook, very easy to learn game.

Depth: 4
-->  Requires good planning and a medium level of strategy.  Many different paths to winning this game, and I haven't found any one to be too unbalancing or that can't be effectively countered.

Player Interactivity: 3
--> You need to be aware of what your opponent is doing and how you can take advantage of opportunities that he gives you, as well as things you can do to block or frustrate his plans.  However, I can understand some people's complaint that the game can become a little predictable about 2/3 of the way through, as each players' strategy and optimal individual path forward becomes clear and play becomes more solitaire.  There are still some ways to interact and affect your opponent throughout the entire game, but it generally decreases as the game progresses as you focus on maximizing victory points for your particular strategy.

Theme: 3
--> This game is supposed to simulate colonization and exploration in the 15-16th century, but the theme is not strong here.  This is a typical resource management Euro without much to reinforce the setting or feeling of the time it is supposedly emulating, so it's a bit dry.

Fast-Moving: 4
--> The rondel mechanism ensures relatively quick turns (usually, you will choose from one of three available actions).  Rarely prone to analysis paralysis.

Replayability: 4 (with the separately-purchased expansions, otherwise a 3)
--> The sameness of the starting board and the identical ship path to the Nagasaki end game is a negative, as it is a bit repetitive.  However, adding the two expansions adds some welcome variety to the game and goes a long way to prevent the game from getting stale.

RATING: 6
As a two-player game, which is what I've tried, the game was a very enjoyable experience and a wonderful variation on the rondel mechanism. The game was a bit too zero-sum for my tastes, and some of the calculations overly time-consuming; however, despite these flaws, I still found the game a tense and exciting game of optimization and timing. I especially enjoy, now in retrospect, the choice of when to open up each new segment of the map -- which can in turn have a number of ripple effects throughout the game depending on your strategy and where your opponents are located relative to your progress. Overall, I look forward to repeat plays, especially with three and four players.

UPDATE: After another game, this time with four players, I have reduced my rating. It's not that the game failed to deliver. Rather, it's flaws were on display. Compared with the more refined (and similar) system presented in Concordia, Navegador feels too much like a mathematical optimization exercise. Perhaps as I gain familiarity with the subtitles of the system, my opinion will change again for the better. As of now, however, I'll stick to Antike for my rondel and Concordia for my rondel-like economy game.

RATING: 9

I finally got to try a 2 player game and it was as fantastic as everybody says. I love the board and bits, and game play really works.  I like all of Mac Gerdts' Rondel games, and he's really getting it down. They're so easy to learn to teach, because all you need to know is how each action on the Rondel is played, and through play the game begins to make sense until you reach an epiphony and you understand the game utterly.  It makes me want to get a whole bunch of people to play nothing but Hamburgum and Antike and Navegador, etc. I'm so glad to have all of MG's really great games in my collection. I taught and played in an amazing game of this at ConnCON 2011 where players made the economy work to be getting 600+ Crusados on their Market action. We all thought the richest player, who also had founded 14 colonies was going to win, but another player sneaked by who was working on 2 strategies. Great game!!

I played another great 5 player game of this at Oasis of Fun in Atlanta this year. I taught and ended up winning in a close 3-way competition. Everybody loves this game, which is now a "dime" for this year's 5 and dime list!

see: Antike, Hamburgum and expansions, Princes of Machu Picchu, Imperial, Imperial 2030.

Amazingly it didn't come out in 2012 at all. Will have to rectify that for 2013!

RATING: 4
What a disappointment. I was really hoping for more in this Gerdt's design. At least the ratings had me all hyped up. The game is a navigation and exploration game, but by the time I reached Goa, I had decided to sell my ships and be a factory manager instead. The last third of the game was rote, predictable and utterly repetitive. In other words, not much fun! As a fellow gamer and friend noted, the game has a weak story arc and does not do the theme much justice.  There is engine building, but subtle. I chose to focus on factories not because I wanted to, but because I sort of fell in the trap of generating income, buying buildings and privileges, then back to getting income..etc. Any deviation from that cycle would just set me back as the rondel made sure that I would spend several rounds just to put myself back in the appropriate position. Also, the market track is clever but fiddly. Moving the cubes up and down the track and remembering which direction the cubes go is mildly annoying. The components are decent and the board is gorgeously printed, but otherwise, the game was a disappointment.

RATING: 9
This game is awesome. I knew I would like this after I read a few reviews, but didn't know that I would love it as much as I do. I love the fact that there is very little luck, and the Rondel makes many decisions agonizing, as it is often extremely difficult to decide whether to skip a space in order to get back to a certain action faster, or to take an action that does not move you as far, but will help on future turns. The mechanics are well executed, as there are many different paths to victory, and a huge amount of strategy and variability, despite the low luck element. I especially enjoy the strategy of the free sailing action. I love Navegador, but never pronounce it like 'Navigator'. What fun is that?

RATING: 4
Another game where the best strategy is to do what no one else is doing, then gather the prestige (bonus scoring) tokens corresponding to this least-traveled path to cement your victory.  And yes, it IS more complicated than that - but not by much.

Note to the general public and/or my wife: Please wake me when this style of game retires to the annals of history.

Frankly, I despise what I see as a severe flaw in economic engine games (London, Navegador, etc.): A given player's viability is highly correlated with the endgame trigger and whether that player is able to perform the last 1-2 actions that might result in a win.  If two actions would win a player the game, but the player is only able to enact one final action, then the actual winner's victory is weak at best and undeserved at worst.  

Whether players get an equal number of turns is also at issue, of course, but there's no guarantee of that here.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Off of two plays... I'm still amazed at how enamored I am with this game. The use of the rondel mechanic works in such an elegant way, and how the beginning of the game feels like a slow build to an eventual race.

While there was some concern after the first play that the game could feel "samey" over time, I don't think that really would be the case as the game setup has enough variables to negate that. The initial tile placement for one game could create the conditions for an all out sprint or a longer grind. Does someone's calculated risk of jumping up the rondel block where you wanted to go? Did your opponent manipulate the market in such a way that you will no longer be able to afford the upgrades that you desperately needed? Or, did you someone use the navegador card before you to swoop in a settle the lands that you were hoping to on your turn?

For a game that is fairly simple rule-wise, there's so many things going on and so many things to take in to account. You really need to take in to account what your opponents are doing on many fronts. Not only because they could block you, but also because you want to position yourself to be able to take advantage on the market when they manipulated. This ebb and flow really adds a great dynamic to the game.

Again... I don't know what it is specifically about this game that I enjoy so much, but it was something that I knew I wanted to have after the first game I played.

RATING: 8.5
Egy másik kedvenc. :meeple: Ez egy felfedezős stratégiai játék. Ami nagyon kiemelkedő ebben a játékban, hogy nagyon jól súlyozott. 

Több taktikai megoldással lehet nyerni (sok felfedezés, gyarmatosítás, gyárépítés). :ninja: Ez egy olyan játék, ahol mindenre szükség van (pénz, munkás, gyarmatok, kiváltságok, épületek...), ezért azt se tudja az ember mit lenne jó csinálni, ami folyamatos feszültséget teremt az emberben. 

Ketten is izgalmas. Mindenkinek ajánlom, aki szereti az összetett stratégiát. :cool:
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of the rare games that rewards specialization. The only reason why I didn't rate it perfect 10 is the fact that shipyard / church privileges are absolutely necessary. Also some sound complains by others that it is always the same (going to the East) but that is correct only for sailing aspect. Also - market tends to lock at highs or lows after several rounds.
Beautiful art, wonderful feeling, rondel is my favorite mechanic and it feels different enough from Imperial and other uses. Maybe it is even a quintessential euro for my taste - fast with rondel, stimulates specialization, has a small functioning market, beautiful board... Maybe some special powers would be interesting, but it would ruin elegance we love in Gerdts' designs.
Sadly under-appreciated in my group.
